        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202315318')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '209eb85c-e4c7-41d4-923f-1e51d8b07bf0'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d175268242" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>A typical polyanionic based material Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F) attracts much interest as a cathode for large-scale sodium-ion batteries in consideration of its stable structure and remarkable energy density. Nevertheless, the large coulombic attraction and repulsion suffered by the mobile Na<sup>+</sup> from structural anions and surrounding Na<sup>+</sup>, respectively, result in a torpid reaction kinetics and inferior rate capability. Herein, Br<sup>−</sup>-doped and Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy preinstalled Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F&nbsp;is prepared to dilute the charges on and inside the Na<sup>+</sup> transportation tunnel. In virtue of density functional theory analysis, Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F reveals a reduction in the bandgap and an increase in electronic conductivity. Meanwhile, the almost electrostatically shielded tunnel in Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F alleviates the coulombic hindrance imposed on Na<sup>+</sup> during its (de)intercalation, which demonstrates a Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity about five times higher than that of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F. Consequently, the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode shows a superior rate capacity of 77.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> under 50&nbsp;C and great cycling property corresponding to a high capacity retention of 94.4% over 800 cycles at 10&nbsp;C. The assembled Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//hard-carbon sodium-ion full-cell presents excellent specific energy/power (226&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−</sup><sup>1</sup>@15424.2&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) as well as outstanding long-term cyclic stability over 1000 cycles at 5&nbsp;C.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315318-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315318-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are recognized as a competitive candidate for the next large-scale energy storage devices with respect to the rich sodium resource and its similar electrochemical characteristics compared with lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0001_R_d175268234e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Electrochem. Energy Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater. Exploration Science Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With a higher retardancy perceived in the mobile Na<sup>+</sup> endowed by a larger ionic radius (102&nbsp;vs. 76&nbsp;pm of Li<sup>+</sup>), the commercialization of SIBs closely depends on the design and development of applicable cathode materials with optimized Na<sup>+</sup> storage efficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0002_R_d175268234e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Commun.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To date, the most widely explored cathode materials are intercalation mechanism-based with high practical viability, specifically, polyanionic compounds and layered transition metal oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0003_R_d175268234e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun. Nat. Energy Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Rare Met.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In these intercalation-based cathodes, Na<sup>+</sup> are stabilized in different types of structural cavities created by their respective basic units, i.e., interconnecting tunnels built by conner-sharing metal octahedra MO<sub>6</sub> and polyanionic tetrahedra XO<sub>4</sub> in polyanionic compounds and the interlayer space between edge-sharing metal octahedra MO<sub>6</sub> layers in transition metal oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0002_R_d175268234e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Commun.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0004_R_d175268234e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Throughout the electrochemical process, the transportation of Na<sup>+</sup> is guided by the local electrostatic force from the surrounding environment, which is also identified as one of the major energy constraints impeding the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0005_R_d175268234e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater. Nature">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, revising the structural chemical environment to create an electrostatically shielded Na<sup>+</sup> transportation highway is considered a fundamental while challenging solution to accelerate the electrochemical kinetics of these cathodes.</p>
         
         <p>Among all the intercalation-based cathodes, a representative polyanionic cathode, sodium vanadium fluorophosphate Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F), stands out with its high working potential of ≈3.6 and 4.1&nbsp;V along with theoretical two Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation contributing to a considerable capacity of 130&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0006_R_d175268234e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Mater. Chem. A Small Nano Energy">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Structural-wise, the alternative stacking of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>10</sub>F bioctahedra and PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra built a robust 3D scaffold for the Na<sup>+</sup> to reside and transport in the <i>ab</i> plane. In this two-sodium utilization regime, the sluggish diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> is still the primary cause responsible for the insufficient capacity and inferior rate capability, which dominantly originates from the strong electrostatic hindrance experienced by Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0007_R_d175268234e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Nanoscale">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Detailed theoretical investigation has demonstrated the critical role of Na<sup>+</sup> content in violently disrupting the energy barrier and thus revising the trajectory of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0008_R_d175268234e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Mater. Chem. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to the largely increased repulsion from the additional Na<sup>+</sup> nearby, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier spikes along with the increasing Na<sup>+</sup> content in the fluorophosphate cathode peaking at the fully sodiated state, which is up to about four times compared to that at its desodiated states. Besides the Na<sup>+</sup>-Na<sup>+</sup> repulsion, the Na<sup>+</sup> experiences large coulombic attraction from the framework anions as well. Particularly, the dangling anions O<sup>2−</sup> at the apex of the VO<sub>5</sub>F reveal a great impact on the Na<sup>+</sup> as it directly faces the diffusion tunnel and the variation of whose species tunes the valence state of the vanadium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0009_R_d175268234e641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, to dilute the electron density of the diffusion tunnel, diverse experimental attempts have been carried out as the typical polyanionic framework of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F offers high flexibility in composition design.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0010_R_d175268234e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Small">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, a rare glance has been laid on the cation side of equal importance. Moreover, challenges remain in the minimization of charge density in the diffusion tunnel to protect the Na<sup>+</sup> from both attractive and repulsive interactions.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, by doping Br<sup>−</sup> at the dangling site and preinstalled Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy into the Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F lattice, the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F is successfully prepared by using a one-step chemical vapor replacing (CVR) means. The phase structure, sodium-ion storage property and reaction kinetics are studied systematically. In virtue of the density functional theory (DFT) research, the evolution of the density of states (DOS) and charge density difference are investigated in detail. As revealed, the bandgap reduces 0.18&nbsp;eV in Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F as compared to that of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F, validating an improvement in the electronic conductivity. Moreover, the dilution of electron density at the dangling site is observed at the Br<sup>−</sup> as compared to the original O<sup>2−</sup>, together with the preinstalled Na<sup>+</sup>, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity in Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F has enhanced to almost five times higher, especially at the low-voltage platform region, than that of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F. When applied in SIBs, the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode demonstrates an excellent rate capacity of 77.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;C. In addition, a great cyclic property of high capacity retention of 94.4% above 800 cycles are obtained by this cathode at 10&nbsp;C. By matching with an anode of hard carbon (HC), the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC sodium-ion full device shows outstanding rate property (226&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>@15&nbsp;424.2&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) as well as superior long-term cycling capability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315318-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315318-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F materials are achieved with a hydrothermal reaction and a following thermal treatment. The crystal structure of the as-obtained samples is examined via an X-ray diffraction pattern (XRD) study. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a> (Supporting Information), all the diffraction peaks for the resulted Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F sample match well with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F structure (ICSD No. 245&nbsp;125, <i>I4/mmm</i> space group). Signally, on top of the existing Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F peaks, additional diffraction peaks belonging to NaBr (asterisk mark) are observed from the intermediate sample that underwent the chemical vapor replacing (CVR) process. During this process, the NH<sub>4</sub>Br decomposes to form corrosive NH<sub>3</sub> and HBr gases, which etch into the Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F lattice to achieve the doping of Br<sup>−</sup> at the dangling O<sup>2−</sup>. Meanwhile, a small quantity of Na<sup>+</sup> are etched away from the grain as well, which crystallizes as NaBr impurity leaving Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies in the lattice. Finally, the Br<sup>−</sup>-doped and Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy-rich sample, denoted as Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F, is obtained by washing off the NaBr impurity with DI water (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0001">1a</a>). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0001">1b</a> show the Rietveld refinements of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F, respectively. The Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F displays the lattice parameters of <i>a</i>/<i>b</i> and <i>c</i> of 6.38646 and 10.64161&nbsp;Å, respectively, corresponding to a cell volume of 434.038&nbsp;Å3 (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Comparatively, Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F demonstrates varied degrees of shrinkage in the <i>a</i>/<i>b</i> (6.37431&nbsp;Å) and <i>c</i> (10.63282&nbsp;Å) axes, with a certain degree of shrinkage in volume (432.030&nbsp;Å3) as well. The exact concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy and Br<sup>−</sup> dopant is ascertained by inductively-coupled plasma (ICP, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). From the Na:V ratio of 2.81:2 in Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F versus 2.96:2 in Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F, a Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy of 0.15 per unit cell is calculated. The Br<sup>−</sup> content in Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F is calculated to be 0.616&nbsp;wt%. Thus, the varying degree of reduction in lattice parameters is attributed to the synergy influence of Br<sup>−</sup> doping and Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy with varying amounts. On the one hand, Br<sup>−</sup> doping at the dangling O<sup>2−</sup> position replaces the V═O covalent bond with an elongated V−Br ionic bond, which would cause slight compression in <i>a</i>/<i>b</i> axis and elongation in <i>c</i> axis. This is similar to the Cl<sup>−</sup>-substitution case.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0010_R_d175268234e896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Small">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy causes shrinkage in all <i>a</i>/<i>b</i>/<i>c</i> parameters.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315318-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/97362a86-36b7-459c-b691-0004bb66fab6/adfm202315318-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/97362a86-36b7-459c-b691-0004bb66fab6/adfm202315318-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3d0761d7-1ce1-4ace-a47c-29c28bd2da9d/adfm202315318-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/97362a86-36b7-459c-b691-0004bb66fab6/adfm202315318-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315318-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315318" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic of the acquisition of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F with chemical vapor replacing (CVR) process. b) Rietveld refinement plots of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F. c) Core-level V 2p spectra of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F. d) Br 3d spectra of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F material. SEM images of e) Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and f) Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F materials. g) EDX mapping of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To probe the surface chemistry of the Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) is used. By showing the XPS survey in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a> (Supporting Information), the information of Na, V, O, P, and F are remarkable for Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F, indicating the existence of the above elements in both samples. Beyond that, the signal of elemental Br is observed in Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F, confirming the existence of Br in Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a> (Supporting Information) shows the core-level Na 1s spectra of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F, in which only one peak at ≈1071.4&nbsp;eV is exhibited for both samples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0011_R_d175268234e1031" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The doublet peaks in the core-level V 2p spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0001">1c</a>) for both Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F samples reveal the similar binding energies of 524.4 and 517.2&nbsp;eV (524.5 and 517.3&nbsp;eV for Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F), belonging to V<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and V<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0012_R_d175268234e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A J. Mater. Chem. A J. Eur. Ceram. Soc. J. Eur. Ceram. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A slight elevation in binding energy of V 2p spectra for Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F may ascribable to the absence of Na<sup>+</sup>. The core level of O 1s (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information) can be resolved into three peaks of O<sub>Ad</sub> (Adsorptive O), P−O and V−O.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0013_R_d175268234e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Electrochim. Acta">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Comparatively, the relative amount of V−O in Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F (46.6%) is less than that of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F (47.8%). Moreover, the strengthening of the V−O bond at higher binding energy in Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F (530.9&nbsp;eV) is detected than that in Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F (530.6&nbsp;eV), which is consistent with the oxidation state of vanadium. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0001">1d</a> shows the 3d core level of Br, in which the Br 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, and Br 3d<sub>5/2</sub> can be matched at 72.1 and 69.7&nbsp;eV, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0014_R_d175268234e1119" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Chem. A">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The morphology of the resulting materials is examined through a scanning electron microscope (SEM). The Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F exhibits a slightly compressed cubic-like structure ≈800&nbsp;nm in size (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0001">1e</a>). After experiencing severe etching during the CVR process, the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F demonstrates an aggregation of smaller particles with irregular structures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0001">1f</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0001">1g</a> shows the corresponding elemental mapping images of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F, confirming the well-proportioned dispersion of Na, O, V, F, P, and Br.</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the electrochemical behavior of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathodes for sodium-ion storage, cyclic voltammetry (CV) is executed first in a sodium-ion half-cell. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a,b</a> (Supporting Information) shows the starting three cycles of CV with 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> for Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathodes, respectively, in which two redox pairs at ≈3.6 and 4.0&nbsp;V are observable for both cathodes. The two redox pairs indicate two-step insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> and reversible V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couple.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0015_R_d175268234e1188" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Small">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The three almost overlapping CV curves reveal high stability and reversibility of the electrochemical process for both cathodes. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0002">2a</a> shows a comparison of the 3<sup>rd</sup> CV of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> between Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathodes. The Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode demonstrates overpotentials of 148 and 85&nbsp;mV for the low-voltage and high-voltage peak pair, respectively, which are smaller than those of the Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F cathode (264 and 89&nbsp;mV). During the redox process, the transportation of Na<sup>+</sup> is dominantly hindered by the electrostatic interaction from surrounding species, i.e., attractive forces from the adjacent anions on the framework and the repulsive forces from the surrounding Na<sup>+</sup>. The Br<sup>−</sup> substitution alleviates the coulombic attraction on Na<sup>+</sup>, meanwhile, the Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies lower the coulombic repulsion on Na<sup>+</sup>. Thus, as reflected in the CV results dominantly in the first redox couple, the reaction kinetics of the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode is greatly improved. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0002">2b</a> display the galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) profiles for Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode, respectively, under a series of current densities, i.e., 0.2−50&nbsp;C. Both cathodes exhibit two highly symmetric platforms at ≈3.6 and 4.0&nbsp;V, which is coincident with the CV curves. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0002">2c</a> compares the GCD profiles of two electrodes under 0.2&nbsp;C, where the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode demonstrates significantly reduced polarization for the 3.6&nbsp;V voltage platform (19&nbsp;vs 45&nbsp;mV for Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F cathode).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315318-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b40fddcd-3f0c-463f-81da-9f07f01aae46/adfm202315318-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b40fddcd-3f0c-463f-81da-9f07f01aae46/adfm202315318-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/015b8413-1e27-4bba-bd60-1e694d809b8b/adfm202315318-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b40fddcd-3f0c-463f-81da-9f07f01aae46/adfm202315318-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315318-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315318" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) A comparation of the CV curves between Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F. b) GCD curves of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F. c) A comparation of the GCD profiles for Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F. d) Rate capacity and e) a comparison of rate capacity of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F with near-term polyanionic-type cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0016" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0016_R_d175268234e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Energy Chem. Adv. Mater. Nano Res. J. Energy Chem. Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> f) Cycling property at 1&nbsp;C of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F. g) A long-term cycling performance of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode at 10&nbsp;C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The rate capability of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathodes is carried out at 0.2−50&nbsp;C. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0002">2d</a>, the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode displays a high specific capacity of 124.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> under 0.2&nbsp;C, which is slightly above the value of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F cathode (114.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). When larger current densities of 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10&nbsp;C are adopted, remarkable specific capacities of 124.5, 123.4, 121.2, 115.9, and 109.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are obtained for Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode. As the current density further increases to 20, 30, 40, and even 50&nbsp;C, considerable specific capacities of 98.9, 91.0, 83.8, and 77.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are still acquired by Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode. By returning the current density to the starting value of 0.2&nbsp;C, almost the same capacity of 124.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is regained for Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F. As a comparison, the Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F cathode only delivers a fairly low capacity of 19.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;C, and a limited capacity of 112.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is recovered when the current density returned to 0.2&nbsp;C. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0002">2e</a> compares the rate capacity of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode with recently reported polyanionic-type sodium vanadium fluorophosphate (NVPF)-based cathode materials for SIBs. The Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode demonstrates excellent rate properties among the major NVPF-based cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0016_R_d175268234e1437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Energy Chem. Adv. Mater. Nano Res. J. Energy Chem. Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cyclic stability of both cathodes is also evaluated through the GCD test at 1&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0002">2f</a>). Over 100 cycles, the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode still releases a striking capacity of 119.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a high capacity retention of 98.8%. Nevertheless, a much lower capacity retention of 90.9% is obtained by Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0002">2g</a> shows the long-term cycling capability of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F under 10&nbsp;C, in which noteworthy capacity retention of 94.4% with an extremely low capacity damping of 0.007% per cycle (800 cycles) is still acquired for this cathode. Therefore, the Br<sup>−</sup> doping and Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy synergistically boost Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode with high rate capacity and long-term cycling performance, which has been compared with the previously reported fluorophosphates and other polyanionic-based cathodes and revealed first class performance (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The excellent capacity of sodium-ion storage at high current density for Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode is principally ascribe to the great improvement of reaction kinetics compared to Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F cathode. Therefore, the electrochemical kinetics of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathodes are further investigated by using CV and GITT techniques. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0003">3a,c</a> displays the CV scans of 0.1−0.8&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> for Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F, respectively. The linear relationship of the peak current versus the square root of scan rates demonstrates a diffusion-controlled reaction for both Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0003">3b,d</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0003">3e</a> compares the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>CV, Na+</sub>) of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathodes calculated from CV scans. The D<sub>CV, Na+</sub> of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode for the peak of A1, A2, C1, and C2 are 4.88&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup>, 11.61&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup>, 1.61&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup>, and 6.19&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are 3.85, 7.08, 4.03, and 4.91 times higher than those of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0003">3f</a> shows the GITT profile and the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>GITT, Na+</sub>) for Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathodes (one single GITT profile and corresponding linear fitting of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathodes are demonstrated in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5,S6</a>, Supporting Information, respectively). The D<sub>GITT, Na+</sub> for Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F cathode fluctuates within a wide range from 10<sup>−13</sup> to 10<sup>−12</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> and the values at the 3.6&nbsp;V plateau are more than two times lower than those at the 4.0&nbsp;V plateau. Whereas, the D<sub>GITT, Na+</sub> for Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode stays at the same level for both plateaus, which are almost five times larger than those of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F for the 3.6&nbsp;V plateau. Obviously, the Br<sup>−</sup> doping and Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy preinstalling together improve Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F structure, hence achieving fast reaction kinetics and high-rate Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability in the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315318-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4f21a242-8ead-4635-a2be-738c83894f80/adfm202315318-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4f21a242-8ead-4635-a2be-738c83894f80/adfm202315318-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2e312688-ec87-4f37-866d-ac7a3e618dfc/adfm202315318-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4f21a242-8ead-4635-a2be-738c83894f80/adfm202315318-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315318-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315318" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">CV curves and the linear relationship of the peak current versus scan rate<sup>1/2</sup> of a,b) Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and c,d) Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F. e) The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F calculated from CV results. f) The GITT profiles as well as the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further investigate the effect of Br<sup>−</sup> doping and Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy in Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F, the density functional theory (DFT) technology is conducted. Showing the density of states (DOS) of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0004">4a</a>, the states close to the Fermi level are principally contributed from the V 3d and O (PO<sub>4</sub>) 2p orbitals. The region near the bottom of the conduction band is principally from the V 3d and dangling O 2p orbitals. The bandgap of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F is calculated to be 2.33&nbsp;eV. Meanwhile, there is a large number of electron transference between V and dangling O, indicating a strong bonding interaction formed between V and dangling O (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0004">4b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a–c</a>, Supporting Information). When taking off a small amount of Na<sup>+</sup> to generate Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy in Na<sub>3-y</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F (y&nbsp;=&nbsp;1/8), in-gap states appear close to the bottom of the conduction band, which are principally contributed from V 3d and dangling O 2p orbitals (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information). The bandgap of Na<sub>3-y</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F slightly reduces to 2.21&nbsp;eV with a slightly enhanced conductivity. The electron density of nearby framework anions does not change significantly upon the generation of Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7d–f,S8b</a>, Supporting Information). When replacing a small amount of dangling O<sup>2−</sup> in Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F with Br<sup>−</sup> to generate Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;1/8), the edge near the Fermi level is slightly revised with the contribution from Br p orbitals on top of those from V 3d and O (in PO<sub>4</sub>) 2p orbitals (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0004">4c</a>). The in-gap states remain with their constitution from the V 3d and dangling O 2p orbitals almost unchanged. The conduction band edge of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F shifts toward the valence band with a further reduced bandgap of 2.15&nbsp;eV, indicating the electrons are easier to be motivated from the valence band to the conduction band and the electronic conductivity is further increased. The enhancement in conductivity is further verified through a four-point probe method, where a 1.6 times higher conductivity is validated in Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F as compared to that of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F (4.3&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;vs. 6.9&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;S&nbsp;m<sup>−1</sup> for Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F, respectively, the calculated details are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The bond between V<sup>4+</sup> and the dangling Br<sup>−</sup> is significantly weakened as indicated by the reduced electron transference, leaving a nearly electrically neutral region (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0004">4d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7g–i</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, the electrostatic hindrance received by Na<sup>+</sup>, i.e., the coulombic attraction from surrounding Na<sup>+</sup> and the coulombic repulsion from dangling anions, significantly weakens after the introduction of Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies and Br<sup>−</sup>, which is conjectured the primary cause for the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315318-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f696e48d-3217-47d9-ad80-fa4ee1cd956b/adfm202315318-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f696e48d-3217-47d9-ad80-fa4ee1cd956b/adfm202315318-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6f1d3aef-a6d1-4da1-abf0-2e2500f6159e/adfm202315318-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f696e48d-3217-47d9-ad80-fa4ee1cd956b/adfm202315318-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315318-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315318" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The DOS and sliced isosurface of a,b) Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F and c,d) Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F. e) In situ XRD pattern of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode recorded in the first cycle (0.2&nbsp;C).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The structural evolution of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode during Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation process is also investigated by using in-situ XRD technology without interval for the initial cycle at 0.2&nbsp;C. As shown in the collected results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0004">4e</a>, obvious variation is observed during Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation, especially for the (101), (002), (200), (103), and (202) planes. The diffraction signal of the (002) plane located at 16.8° gradually transfers to a lower degree during Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation stage and merges with the (101) peak to form a new signal along with charging to 4.5&nbsp;V. Similarly, the merging of diffraction signals also occurs for (200) and (103) planes. In addition, the peak of the (202) plane at 32.9° gradually shifts to a higher degree during Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation. In the following Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation stage, all of the diffraction peaks for the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode go back to their pristine state, demonstrating a reversible phase transformation occurrence all along the Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation process.</p>
         
         <p>The feasibility of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode in practical application is further evaluated in a sodium-ion full-cell (SIFC). As schematically illustrated in the inset of <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0005">5a</a>, the as-prepared Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F sample and the commercially available hard carbon (HC) are applied as cathode and anode material, respectively, to assemble the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC. The commercial HC material is purchased and used directly without further treatment. The structural and morphological information of HC material and its sodium-ion storage capability in a half-cell are presented in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10,S11</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0005">5a</a> shows the rate capacity of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC (0.2−40 C). When adopting current densities of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 20 and 30 C, discharge capacities as high as 120.7, 120.4, 119.6, 118.1, 114.3, 107.3, 93.2, and 80.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are achieved by Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC, respectively. Even under 40&nbsp;C, the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC still releases a laudable capacity of 75.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0005">5b</a> exhibits the corresponding GCD curves of 0.2−40 C with similar shapes even at 40 C further confirming the high reversibility of this SIFC. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0005">5c</a> illustrates the leading role of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC in terms of energy/power density among the recently reported SIFC based on vanadium-base polyanionic-type cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0010_R_d175268234e2003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Small">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0017_R_d175268234e2003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC can release an amazing specific energy of 446.5&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> under the specific power of 98.7&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (0.2&nbsp;C). Even with an ultrahigh specific power of 15424.2&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>, a considerable energy density of 226&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> is still achieved by Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC. The long cycling stability of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC is also tested at 5&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0005">5d</a>). A high discharge capacity of 107.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is obtained by Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC for 1000 cycles, corresponding to a high capacity retention of 84.9% as well as an astonishingly low capacity damping rate of 0.085% per cycle. Besides, a quite high average coulombic efficiency (CE) of 99.6% is achieved by Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC. As depicted in the inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315318-fig-0005">5d</a>, an array of LED bulbs arranged into an “NPU” pattern are successfully lit up by the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC demonstrating its practicability further.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315318-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/682e9cf2-e585-4f8a-bd43-13cd9fd2dcfd/adfm202315318-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/682e9cf2-e585-4f8a-bd43-13cd9fd2dcfd/adfm202315318-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/82ca3240-d055-4a32-90fa-039faa9e7b68/adfm202315318-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/682e9cf2-e585-4f8a-bd43-13cd9fd2dcfd/adfm202315318-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315318-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315318" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rate capacity (0.2−40&nbsp;C with initial CE of 86.8%, inset shows a schematic illustration of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC) and b) corresponding GCD profiles of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC. c) A comparison of specific energy/power of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC with other SIBs full devices.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0010" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0010_R_d175268234e2103" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Small">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0017" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0017_R_d175268234e2103" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano Adv. Mater. ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> d) Cycling performance of Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC SIFC at 5&nbsp;C (inset shows the digital image of the LED array powered by Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F//HC full cell).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315318-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315318-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, by introducing Br<sup>−</sup> and generating Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy, dual regulation on dangling anions and Na<sup>+</sup> sites in Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F is successfully achieved to obtain Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F. DFT investigation reveals that the bandgap is greatly reduced through Br<sup>−</sup> doping and Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy generation in Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F, which increases its electronic conductivity. In addition, the doped Br<sup>−</sup> weakens the coulombic attraction of movable Na<sup>+</sup> suffered from dangling anions and promotes Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. Therefore, as a cathode for SIBs, the Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F electrode exhibits an excellent rate property of 77.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;C and great cyclic capability of high capacity retention of 94.4% for 800 cycles at 10&nbsp;C. Moreover, the sodium-ion full devices assembled with Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F cathode and HC anode demonstrate superior energy/power density (226&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>@15&nbsp;424.2&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) as well as outstanding cycling performance over 1000 cycles at 5&nbsp;C. The present study provides a span-new strategy with multiple-angle regulation in polyanionic-type cathode cathodes simultaneously to accelerate the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and achieve high-rate sodium-ion storage capability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315318-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315318-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315318-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315318-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315318-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315318-sec-0070-title"> Materials Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>Preparation of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F): First, 1.512&nbsp;g H<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>∙2H<sub>2</sub>O and 0.728&nbsp;g V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> were added to DI water (60&nbsp;mL) and following for stirring with 1&nbsp;h under 70&nbsp;°C. Then, 0.424&nbsp;g Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, 0.92&nbsp;g NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, and 0.168&nbsp;g NaF were put in the above navy-blue solution and maintained stirring with an additional 0.5&nbsp;h. Afterward, the resulted reaction liquid was transferred into a hydrothermal reactor (100&nbsp;mL) and maintained for 24&nbsp;h under 140&nbsp;°C. The sediment was gathered with centrifugation and washed three times by DI water. After drying, the last powder sample of Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F was obtained.</p>
                  
                  <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315318-sec-0080">
                     
                     <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section5" id="adfm202315318-sec-0080-title"> Preparation of Br<sup>−</sup> Doping and Na<sup>+</sup> Vacancy-Driven Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (Na<sub>3−y</sub>VPO<sub>2−x</sub>Br<sub>x</sub>F)</h4>
                     
                     <p>Two hundred milligrams of NH<sub>4</sub>Br was dissolving into 40&nbsp;mL DI water. Then, 100&nbsp;mg of the obtained Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F sample was dispersed into the above NH<sub>4</sub>Br solution and stirred until evaporation of all water. The residual mixed solid was ground and annealed at 400&nbsp;°C for 1&nbsp;h within a tube furnace with Ar atmosphere. Finally, the collected sediment was washed with DI water for only one time and dried for the final sample. The control sample was obtained via annealing the Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F sample without any other process.</p>
                     </section>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315318-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315318-sec-0090-title"> Materials Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The phase and crystal structure of the obtained samples was detected through using X-ray diffraction (XRD, Bruker D8). And the chemical property on the surface of the resulting samples was ascertained via X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, PHI VersaProbe III). Scanning electron microscopy (SEM, FEI nanoSEM 450) was applied to examine the morphological difference of the as-prepared samples.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315318-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315318-sec-0100-title"> Electrochemical Evaluation</h4>
                  
                  <p>The resulted Na<sub>3</sub>VPO<sub>2</sub>F materials (70&nbsp;wt%) were combined with conductive black (20&nbsp;wt%), CMC (10&nbsp;wt%) and DI water, stirring at room temperature with 10&nbsp;h. The resultant mixture was spread over an Al plate and vacuum-dried for 12&nbsp;h under 110&nbsp;°C. Then the active materials loaded Al plate was punched into a circular shape (<i>ϕ</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;12&nbsp;mm) for the target electrodes with a mass in the range of 1.0−1.2&nbsp;mg. The button cells (CR2032) were used to package the half and full cells in an argon atmosphere glovebox. The Na plates (HC anode in full cell), fiberglass, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> organic solution (solvent: PC:EC&nbsp;=&nbsp;1:1 with volume ratio, including 5&nbsp;wt% FEC) were applied as the counter electrodes, separators, and electrolyte, respectively. An electrochemical workstation (IVIUMnSTAT) was applied to proceed cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurement. The battery testing equipment (Neware, BTS 7.5 x) was used to assess the rate and cyclic capability with the galvanostatic charge/discharge test. The mass of active material in the cathode was used for the calculation of capacity.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315318-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315318-sec-0110-title"> Computational Methods</h4>
                  
                  <p>The density functional theory (DFT) technology was adopted to execute the theoretical calculation with the initio simulation package (VASP) of the Vienna.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0018_R_d175268234e2324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Phy. Rev. B">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The connection of the nucleus and extranuclear electron was described by applying the projector augmented wave (PAW) way.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0019_R_d175268234e2329" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Phy. Rev. B">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Through the generalized gradient approximation (GGA) of Perdew-Burke-Ernzerhof (PBE) the exchange-correlation potentials were realized.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0020_R_d175268234e2334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Phy. Rev. Lett.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Spin polarizations were considered in all calculations. The cut-off energy was set as the value of 520&nbsp;eV. Convergence criterion of energy and force were set as 1&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−6</sup>&nbsp;eV and 0.02&nbsp;eV&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In addition, a 2&nbsp;×&nbsp;2&nbsp;×&nbsp;2 Monkhorst-Pack mesh with K-point sampling of Brillouin-zone integrals was employed for achieving geometric optimization, meanwhile 4&nbsp;×&nbsp;4&nbsp;×&nbsp;5 for the calculation of electronic properties. The DFT+U means was employed in the calculation by setting the U as 4&nbsp;eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315318-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315318-bib-0021_R_d175268234e2344" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Phy. Rev. Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
